                         <div id="articleHeader">
    <div id="main-article-info">
        <h1>The dubious joys of perving over fellow passengers online</h1>
        <p id="stand-first" class="stand-first-alone">A too-casual approach to the posting of pictures of men on TubeCrush seems a bit off, but it's true women put up with worse</p>
    </div>
</div>

                         <div id="content"><ul class="article-attributes"><li> <a href="http://www.guardian.co.uk/profile/sunnyhundal"> <img class="contributor-pic-small" src="http://static.guim.co.uk/sys-images/Guardian/Pix/pictures/2008/05/07/sunny_hundal_140x140.jpg" width="60" height="60" alt="Sunny Hundal" title="Contributor picture"/> </a> </li> <li id="contrib-shift"><ul> <li class="byline"> <a class="contributor" href="http://www.guardian.co.uk/profile/sunnyhundal"> Sunny Hundal</a> </li> <li class="publication"> <a href="http://www.guardian.co.uk/">guardian.co.uk</a>, <time datetime="2011-04-19T16:43BST">Tuesday 19 April 2011 16.43 BST</time> </li> </ul></li> </ul> 
<div id="article-wrapper" data-global-auto-refresh-switch="on">
<div id="main-content-picture"><img src="http://static.guim.co.uk/sys-images/Guardian/Pix/pictures/2011/4/19/1303226581919/Crowded-tube-007.jpg" width="460" height="276" alt="Crowded tube"/> <div class="caption">TubeCrush &#8230; the website encourages women to send in pictures of attractive men on the tube. Photograph: Alamy</div></div>
<div id="article-body-blocks"><p>It was a light-hearted story, but something wasn't quite right about it. Last night I tweeted a link to <a href="http://www.thisislondon.co.uk/lifestyle/article-23942322-who-is-your-tube-crush.do" title="Evening Standard: Who is your tube crush">this news story</a> about a group of friends who have set up a website called TubeCrush that encourages women to send in pictures (mostly of men) travelling on London's tube. They can then choose to rate them on a sliding scale. I said in <a href="http://twitter.com/sunny_hundal/status/60127202947252224" title="Twitter: sunny_hundal">my tweet</a>: "Erm, is it just me or if this site was about women, people would be getting arrested right about now?"</p><p/><p/><p>Before I recount the huge response, I should explain myself. What struck me was the casual way that the Evening Standard reported on the story in a "oh look, cool young things have set up a website so we can perve on each other" type of way. Maybe it was just an extremely slow news day. But if the sexes had been reversed, would it have been seen so benign? Probably not. I just found the Evening Standard's casual attitude a bit off.</p><p/><p>Some agreed: <a href="http://twitter.com/#!/HelenWayte/status/60127748257095680" title="Twitter: HelenWayte">women</a> and <a href="http://twitter.com/#!/DoxieVee/status/60127654992560128" title="Twitter: DoxieVee">men</a>. Andy <a href="http://twitter.com/wibblenut/status/60128880505257984" title="Twitter: wibblenut">pointed out</a> that no crime was being committed, "it's just creepy, whether men or women do it". Jennifer <a href="http://twitter.com/jaomahony/status/60135392137068544" title="Twitter: jaomahony">said</a> it was a form of stalking.</p><p/><p>But all this raises a few issues. First, women have to put up with far worse. As Hanna <a href="http://twitter.com/hannathomas/status/60135926076145664" title="Twitter: hannathomas">pointed out</a>, "there are loads of sites out there about women. Upskirt shots, no less. No one gets arrested". Soph <a href="http://twitter.com/sophwarnes/status/60136917668343808" title="Twitter: sophwarnes">said</a> the comparison was ridiculous: "Men get photos taken of them and are whinging about it. No offence but I get stared at/talked to/touched all the time by men."</p><p/><p>MyCrippledEagle <a href="http://mycrippledeagle.wordpress.com/2011/04/19/objectification-power-and-some-men-being-twats/" title="My Crippled Ego: Objectification, Power and Some Men Being Twats">blogged</a> in response:</p><p/><p><blockquote>"If a woman takes a picture of a man on a train and he sees her, one or both will be embarrassed but very few men would feel threatened by such behaviour. If, however, a man takes a picture of a woman on a train and she sees him, immediately she has to think about the possible dangers of the situation. Is this guy a creep? Is he a potential rapist? Is he going to follow up the action with some verbal or physical harassment?"</blockquote></p><p/><p>Is it unreasonable for women to feel so worried? If you look at the <a href="http://www.dh.gov.uk/en/MediaCentre/Pressreleases/DH_122115" title="Department of Health: A Woman Raped Every Ten Minutes">statistics for rape</a> and sexual harassment, don't they have every right? Maybe that's why I couldn't work myself into an outrage over it: most men don't face a daily torrent of low-level sexual harassment, so this wasn't seen as a big threat. Alex <a href="http://twitter.com/angryyoungalex/status/60137061319061504" title="Twitter: angryyoungalex">found himself</a> "5% demeaned, 35% flattered, 60% surprised".</p><p/><p>But it's AmieTsang who <a href="http://twitter.com/amietsang/status/60144079593406464" title="Twitter: amietsang">summed up</a> most of the male responses I got: it's all lighthearted banter until you come across a picture of yourself rated as *munter*.</p><p/><p>I've got my long scarf ready.</p></div>
</div>
</div>

I know for a fact that if my son found a pic of himself up on a website like that he would be utterly horrified and ashamed.</p><p>Yesterday evening we were discussing at home how it was that when the internet's predecessors, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/JANET" rel="nofollow">JANET</a> and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/ARPANET" rel="nofollow">ARPANET</a> were first started, people had such high minded-hopes for what it would achieve. I've heard various comments, again mainly from women, suggesting that men 'like it', or are flattered. Well in my experience the only men who don't have a problem with being treated like a sex object and having their rights impinged upon are ones who never experience it. It's a bit like saying because some women choose to display their breasts on Page 3, all women must like being treated like a sex object. That isn't true of women and it is also wrong to lump all men in with the lowest common denominator of our sex.</p><p>I'm disappointed so many feminists have belittled this by focussing on the fact that women have more of this to put up with. Of course they do, but this is more of the same thing, just in reverse. </p><p>I think any feminist who has issues (as I do) with the sexual objectification of women should be the first to back people saying this is unacceptable. Undermining it by pointing out women have it worse is a bit like a man responding to a British feminist that women have it worse in the Middle East. <ul class="comment b2" id="comment-10432726"> <li class="comment-author"> <div class="badges"> <img src="http://resource.guim.co.uk/discussion/static/1673/images/icons_contributor_28.gif" width="28" height="28" alt="Contributor"/> </div> <div class="profile"> <img class="author-profile-picture" src="http://static.guim.co.uk/sys-images/discussion/avatars/2010/09/27/AllyF/193f5e45-703e-4a6b-a52a-7e013008bd57/60x60.png" height="60" width="60" alt=""/> <a href="http://www.guardian.co.uk/discussion/user/AllyF" title="User profile page">AllyF</a> <p class="date">19 April 2011 6:50PM</p> </div> <div class="comment-body"> <p>You know, it is actually possible to take gender out of these debates. Comes as a hell of a shock to most Guardian writers (and perhaps readers) but it is true. </p><p>Is it intrusive and exploitative to photograph someone without their permission and upload it to the internet to be rated 'hot or not?' Yes, it is.</p><p>Are people offended or flattered by being exploited in this way? It depends on the individual. Some will be, some won't be.</p><p>To broaden the issue out, are people made to feel vulnerable, scared, at risk or belittled by being photographed, gawped at, chatted up, insulted or abused in public? Yes, often they are, particularly if it is done in a crude and aggressive way. </p><p>Is it ever acceptable to harass someone, intimidate someone, abuse someone, assault or molest someone? No, it's not. </p><p>To address the point made explicitly by DavidShariatmadari and implicitly by Sunny, are women more likely to be harassed or have unpleasant experiences than men? Yes, they probably are, at least of a sexualised nature. But we have to be very careful not to say that when a man is harassed or has an unpleasant experience this is less significant or less worrisome, and I think that is a line that was firmly crossed by DavidS.</p> </div> </li> <li class="comment-tools"> <ul> <li class="recomended"> <a title="Recommend this comment" class="recommend" onclick="recommendComment(10432726);return false;">Recommend?</a> class="comment b2" id="comment-10441745"> <li class="comment-author"> <div class="badges"> <img src="http://resource.guim.co.uk/discussion/static/1673/images/icons_contributor_28.gif" width="28" height="28" alt="Contributor"/> </div> <div class="profile"> <img class="author-profile-picture" src="http://static.guim.co.uk/sys-images/discussion/avatars/2010/10/01/unexceptional/dc1db275-6ca8-4881-a424-74108c1705a9/60x60.png" height="60" width="60" alt=""/> <a href="http://www.guardian.co.uk/discussion/user/unexceptional" title="User profile page">unexceptional</a> <p class="date">20 April 2011 1:15PM</p> </div> <div class="comment-body"> <p>Fencewalker,</p><blockquote><p><i>That's not to say there's nothing wrong with Tubecrush, although personally I'd have thought there's nothing at all wrong (and everything right) with people appreciating human beauty, so long as it doesn't descend into the flipside, with people getting insulted for not being deemed up to standard. Basically, if its done in a nice way, then I can't see a problem with it.</i></p><p>So many facepalms, but I'll go with this one. Surely it's for the person to decide if they want to be treated in this way? Isn't ignoring that a minor form of 'oppression'?<br/>As for the 'nothing wrong with people appreciating human beauty, so long as...' bit, I'd have thought from the rest of your post (Oppression! Rargh!) that you'd be against Page 3, or the body dysmorphia that can result from society's obsession with physical perfection.* It doesn't need a personally insulting comment to a pic to have that kind of effect on a person.</p><p>* I'm saying this only partly to out-Guardianista you.</p></blockquote><p>I'll try for a spot of Devil's advocacy here.</p><p>Firstly, I'm not sure about the 'oppression' angle: what right do we have reasonably to say that we control our own image in public? If we're on the Tube, then I think it's fair to say we've consented to having our visages espied by the masses. Photography doesn't really seem to be any different to that: I've probably featured in 1,000 tourist pictures this year simply from walking around in public.</p><p>So I'm not sure the issue is about having a picture taken. We all have pictures taken without our knowledge, consent or approval. So what about the issue of consent to having it put on the internet? I'd agree that it's desirable and decent to gain consent before doing it, but that doesn't mean it's wrong not to. Again, I'm pretty sure I've been in the background of a few TV news reports simply by walking by and sticking in my own world. What imperative should they face to gain my consent for broadcasting that? What's the dividing line - should consent only be sought when someone is a major feature of a media piece, and how do we define that? How do I own the rights to my image when I'm displaying it, and have displayed it, indiscriminately? Is there a reasonable expectation of limitation to my image - a size of audience? Cos that site doesn't look like it gets (or got) much traffic; the fucker crashes with every click. Basically, if I've made the choice to go into the world where people and cameras exist and my image can be seen, why and how should I have control over what people do with it or think of it?</p><p>And then, I think, we have the 'purpose' angle. I'm pretty sure there'd be a lot less objection if the pictures were hosted on a site about 'Chaps I believe my grandfather would have gotten on well with'. Is it the fact that the men are sexually rated that causes the problem? Why is it the sexual nature that causes problems? I mean, there are loads of blogs featuring things like 'bald men with bad tattoos' and 'unhappy hipster doofuses' and 'the corpulent patrons of WallMart with their horrific dress sense', but they don't cause the same amount of trouble. Why is it sexuality that causes problems? I'm not sure I see quite why being rated according to fuckability is any worse than anything else - why is that aspect special?</p><p>Then there's the tone of the site's commentary, which strikes me as unpleasant. But that counts for nothing: that arseholes do something is not good reason for condemning it.</p> </div> </li> <li class="comment-tools"> <ul> <li class="recomended"> <a title="Recommend this comment" class="recommend" onclick="recommendComment(10441745);return false;">Recommend?</a> (<span class="recommended" id="recommended-count-10441745">1</span>) </li> <li class="abuse-report"> <a class="report-abuse " href="http://www.guardian.co.uk/discussion/report-abuse/comment/10441745"> Report abuse </a> </li> <li class="clip"> <form action="http://www.guardian.co.uk/users/clippings/add" method="get"> <input type="hidden" name="microappName" value="discussion-main"/> <input type="hidden" name="microappItemId" value="comment-10441745"/> <input class="form-based-login-required package-required-RCO" type="submit" value="Clip" title="Add this comment to your profile bookmarks"/> </form> </li> <li class="link">| <a href="http://www.guardian.co.uk/discussion/comment-permalink/10441745" title="Permalink to this comment" alt="Permalink to this comment"> Link </a> </li> </ul> </li> </ul> <ul class="comment b2" id="comment-10441797"> <li class="comment-author"> <div class="badges"> </div> <div class="profile"> <img class="author-profile-picture" src="http://static.guim.co.uk/sys-images/Guardian/Pix/site_furniture/2010/09/01/no-user-image.gif" height="60" width="60" alt=""/> <a href="http://www.guardian.co.uk/discussion/user/moroboshi" title="User profile page">moroboshi</a> <p class="date">20 April 2011 1:18PM</p> </div> <div class="comment-body"> <p>Kind of surprising really, as women seem far more subtle in their appreciation for the opposite sex. <ul class="comment b2" id="comment-10444500"> <li class="comment-author"> <div class="badges"> </div> <div class="profile"> <img class="author-profile-picture" src="http://static.guim.co.uk/sys-images/Guardian/Pix/site_furniture/2010/09/01/no-user-image.gif" height="60" width="60" alt=""/> <a href="http://www.guardian.co.uk/discussion/user/Proudhon1" title="User profile page">Proudhon1</a> <p class="date">20 April 2011 3:20PM</p> </div> <div class="comment-body"> <p>Surely the fact that women have to put up with this sort of crap (and worse) from a certain type of moronic male does not mean it's OK that men have to as well? </p><p>Two wrongs and all that...</p> </div> </li> <li class="comment-tools"> <ul> <li class="recomended"> <a title="Recommend this comment" class="recommend" onclick="recommendComment(10444500);return false;">Recommend?</a> (<span class="recommended" id="recommended-count-10444500">0</span>) </li> <li class="abuse-report"> <a class="report-abuse " href="http://www.guardian.co.uk/discussion/report-abuse/comment/10444500"> Report abuse </a> </li> <li class="clip"> <form action="http://www.guardian.co.uk/users/clippings/add" method="get"> <input type="hidden" name="microappName" value="discussion-main"/> <input type="hidden" name="microappItemId" value="comment-10444500"/> <input class="form-based-login-required package-required-RCO" type="submit" value="Clip" title="Add this comment to your profile bookmarks"/> </form> </li> <li class="link">| <a href="http://www.guardian.co.uk/discussion/comment-permalink/10444500" title="Permalink to this comment" alt="Permalink to this comment"> Link </a> </li> </ul> </li> </ul> <ul class="comment b2" id="comment-10444596"> <li class="comment-author"> <div class="badges"> </div> <div class="profile"> <img class="author-profile-picture" src="http://static.guim.co.uk/sys-images/Guardian/Pix/site_furniture/2010/09/01/no-user-image.gif" height="60" width="60" alt=""/> <a href="http://www.guardian.co.uk/discussion/user/onlyhappywhenitrains" title="User profile page">onlyhappywhenitrains</a> <p class="date">20 April 2011 3:24PM</p> </div> <div class="comment-body"> <p>TubeCrush does have a takedown request form, and has information about the legalities of taking pictures of people in public - short version, yes it is legal.</p><p>There's several interesting aspects to discuss with regard to TubeCrush.</p><p>One is the overwhelmingly heteronormative reporting on it - the media assume that because the site consists of pictures of men then it <i>must</i> be women taking the pictures and rating them - completely oblivious to the existence of men who find men attractive. </p><p>Another is that it's newsworthy precisely because it is perceived to be women looking at and objectifying men, or because more generally men are not represented often with the focus being on their looks (again, other than in the gay media). It's odd to us because we are so inured to women being objectified, their looks judged, and their worth measured by their attractiveness to men. This is a scrutiny that men usually escape - straight men expect to be doing the looking and judging, not to be subjected to it. </p><p>Sunny and the commenters saying that the scale of the problem of harassment and sexual violence targeted at women greatly outweighs the intrusion and discomfort for a man of having his anonymous picture posted on a website for his looks to admired, are entirely right. The situations are not equivalent. </p><p>The men photographed are sitting or standing on a train, going about their everyday lives. They aren't being spied on in private situations, they're being photographed from a respectful distance. They're fully clothed. Though the picture caption might comment on a particular physical feature, the pictures are all full body or head &amp; upper-body shots - not pictures of body parts focusing on legs or rear or breasts as is typical of objectifying pictures of women. They aren't being asked to pose or to dress any particular way. Most of them are absorbed in looking at their phone or computer or listening to music, and would never know they had been snapped. Bar the context of voyeuristic harassment and stalking being a not uncommon experience for women, if these exact pictures were of women not men, just sitting on the train, there would be little problematic about them. </p><p>The issues of privacy and consent are very important, and the fact that the men photographed aren't asked for consent and have to opt-out retroactively is certainly worth criticising. It is legal to take pictures of people in public but taking deliberate photographs of an individual person who you don't know (as opposed to them happening to be in a picture you are taking of something else or of a crowd of people), is not respectful, and can be distressing and threatening. I personally hate being photographed, hate the idea of omnipresent CCTV, and the idea generally of someone taking your picture whether you know about it or not, and having no idea why or what they'll do with it is upsetting. If it were a man doing it I would feel further fear and worry that this might be part of some kind of stalking behaviour not just a one off. </p><p>The other problem with TubeCrush is the normalisation of judging and commenting on strangers' appearances, of the idea that if someone is in public then their presence is some kind of invitation for you to judge them. Yes, we are becoming a voyeuristic culture, but that does not stop us discussing the ethics of this and using the technology in ways that are respectful and which uphold privacy and support the importance of consent.</p><p>But if men find the idea that you might be being photographed by a stranger while sat on the train and then your photo put online to invite comments on your looks discomforting and intrusive, then imagine how much more so is it discomforting and intrusive for someone to interrupt your reading or listening, insist on making you give them your attention, and ignore your signs of reluctance; or to touch you without your consent, or to expose themselves to you, or grope or rub up against you; or to shout at you, or follow you. Again these are common experiences for women. <a href="http://kateharding.net/2009/10/08/guest-blogger-starling-schrodinger%E2%80%99s-rapist-or-a-guy%E2%80%99s-guide-to-approaching-strange-women-without-being-maced/" rel="nofollow">Being hypervigilant and suspicious of pushy men is common sense and self-protectiveness.</a></p><p>Another point, again related to the relatively mild intrusiveness of TubeCrush - expressing a crush on someone is not the same as harrassing them. When feminists challenge the objectifying and harrassing behaviour a response is often that it's just an expression of admiration and attraction. (And in this thread here several male commenters have said things like "I'd love to be sexually objectified"). They are NOT the same things. You can admire and fancy someone without making them feel uncomfortable and without thinking or acting as if you are entitled to their bodies or time or attention, without pushing yourself onto them.</p> </div> </li> <li class="comment-tools"> <ul> <li class="recomended"> <a title="Recommend this comment" class="recommend" onclick="recommendComment(10444596);return false;">Recommend?</a> href="http://www.guardian.co.uk/discussion/user/owaingr" title="User profile page">owaingr</a> <p class="date">20 April 2011 5:32PM</p> </div> <div class="comment-body"> <p>@SivaBhaskaran: I consider myself sympathetic to the ideas of feminism, but I don't really have a problem with objectification. People do it all the time. How can you not? I think the idea is that it depersonalises women in the sense of stripping them of autonomy, and I suppose dignity. I don't really see it that way. The commodification of women does that, but that's rather different. Simply taking pleasure in looking at a woman (assuming you're not being creepy about it) is one of life's better aspects. People check each other out all the time. I'd find a web site dedicated to surreptitious shots of women taken by men rather off-putting. There's a difference between that and simply casually glancing. The other way round doesn't bother me, although it is questionable (but not much). And I think the reason is the intent and the level of menace.</p> </div> </li> <li class="comment-tools"> <ul> <li class="recomended"> <a title="Recommend this comment" class="recommend" onclick="recommendComment(10446843);return false;">Recommend?</a> (<span class="recommended" id="recommended-count-10446843">0</span>) </li> <li class="abuse-report"> <a class="report-abuse " href="http://www.guardian.co.uk/discussion/report-abuse/comment/10446843"> Report abuse </a> </li> <li class="clip"> <form action="http://www.guardian.co.uk/users/clippings/add" method="get"> <input type="hidden" name="microappName" value="discussion-main"/> <input type="hidden" name="microappItemId" value="comment-10446843"/> <input class="form-based-login-required package-required-RCO" type="submit" value="Clip" title="Add this comment to your profile bookmarks"/> </form> </li> <li class="link">| <a href="http://www.guardian.co.uk/discussion/comment-permalink/10446843" title="Permalink to this comment" alt="Permalink to this comment"> Link </a> </li> </ul> </li> </ul> <ul class="comment b2" id="comment-10447154"> <li class="comment-author"> <div class="badges"> </div> <div class="profile"> <img class="author-profile-picture" src="http://static.guim.co.uk/sys-images/discussion/avatars/2010/08/23/Gipserio/f645eefb-160a-4d96-91e2-05a19cec2043/60x60.png" height="60" width="60" alt=""/> <a href="http://www.guardian.co.uk/discussion/user/Gipserio" title="User profile page">Gipserio</a> <p class="date">20 April 2011 5:58PM</p> </div> <div class="comment-body"> <blockquote><p>ranslated<br/>19 April 2011 5:20PM<br/>Glad to see I made it onto Tubecrush.
